<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Natural gas</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Gaseous fossil fuel</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gas-natural.jpg"><img alt="Close-up image of a natural gas burner on a stove showing the characteristic blue hue of a natural gas flame." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Gas-natural.jpg/300px-Gas-natural.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Gas-natural.jpg/450px-Gas-natural.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/06/Gas-natural.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gas-natural.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Natural gas burning on a gas stove</div></div></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Liquefied_petroleum_gas" title="Liquefied petroleum gas">liquefied petroleum gas</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chuhuo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Chuhuo.jpg/300px-Chuhuo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Chuhuo.jpg/450px-Chuhuo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Chuhuo.jpg/600px-Chuhuo.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chuhuo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Burning of natural gas coming out of the ground in <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a></div></div></div><p><b>Natural gas</b> (also called <b>fossil gas</b> or simply <b>gas</b>) is a naturally occurring mixture of gaseous <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> consisting primarily of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> in addition to various smaller amounts of other higher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkanes" title="Alkanes">alkanes</a>. Usually low levels of trace gases like <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> are also present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Natural gas is colorless and odorless, so <a href="/wiki/Odorizer" title="Odorizer">odorizers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Methanethiol" title="Methanethiol">mercaptan</a>, which smells like sulfur or rotten eggs, are commonly added to natural gas supplies for safety so that leaks can be readily detected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Natural gas is a <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Non-renewable_resource" title="Non-renewable resource">non-renewable resource</a> that is formed when layers of <a href="/wiki/Organic_matter" title="Organic matter">organic matter</a> (primarily marine microorganisms<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>) decompose under <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_digestion" title="Anaerobic digestion">anaerobic</a> conditions and are subjected to intense heat and pressure underground over millions of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eiaex_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-eiaex-4">[4]</a></sup> The energy that the decayed organisms originally obtained from the sun via <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> is stored as chemical energy within the molecules of methane and other hydrocarbons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_ng_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-epa_ng-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Natural gas can be burned for heating, cooking,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Gas-fired_power_plant" title="Gas-fired power plant">electricity generation</a>. It is also used as a chemical feedstock in the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Plastic" title="Plastic">plastics</a> and other commercially important <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic chemicals</a> and less commonly used as a <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_vehicle" title="Natural gas vehicle">fuel for vehicles</a>. 
</p><p>The extraction and consumption of natural gas is a major and growing contributor to <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ieaco2_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-ieaco2-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-owidebf_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-owidebf-9">[9]</a></sup>  Both the gas itself (specifically <a href="/wiki/Methane_emissions" title="Methane emissions">methane</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere">carbon dioxide</a>, which is released when natural gas is burned, are <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AGI_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-AGI-11">[11]</a></sup> When burned for <a href="/wiki/Furnace_(central_heating)" title="Furnace (central heating)">heat</a> or <a href="/wiki/Gas-fired_power_plant" title="Gas-fired power plant">electricity</a>, natural gas emits fewer toxic air pollutants, less carbon dioxide, and almost no particulate matter compared to other fossil and <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> fuels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Gas_venting" title="Gas venting">gas venting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Routine_flaring" title="Routine flaring">flaring</a>, along with unintended <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fugitive_emissions" title="Fugitive emissions">fugitive emissions</a> throughout the <a href="/wiki/Supply_chain" title="Supply chain">supply chain</a>, can result in natural gas having a similar <a href="/wiki/Carbon_footprint" title="Carbon footprint">carbon footprint</a> to other fossil fuels overall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Natural gas can be found in underground <a href="/wiki/Geological_formation" title="Geological formation">geologic formations</a>, often alongside other fossil fuels like <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">oil</a> (petroleum). Most natural gas has been created through either biogenic or thermogenic processes. Biogenic gas is formed when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methanogenic" title="Methanogenic">methanogenic</a> organisms in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marshes" title="Marshes">marshes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bogs" title="Bogs">bogs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landfills" title="Landfills">landfills</a>, and shallow sediments anaerobically decompose but are not subjected to high temperatures and pressures. Thermogenic gas takes a much longer period of time to form and is created when organic matter is heated and compressed deep underground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eiaex_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-eiaex-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="impurities"></span>
During petroleum production, natural gas is sometimes <a href="/wiki/Gas_flare" title="Gas flare">flared</a> rather than being collected and used. Before natural gas can be burned as a fuel or used in manufacturing processes, it almost always has to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_gas_processing" title="Natural gas processing">processed</a> to remove impurities such as water. The byproducts of this processing include <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Butane" title="Butane">butanes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pentane" title="Pentane">pentanes</a>, and higher molecular weight hydrocarbons. Hydrogen sulfide (which may be converted into pure <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>), <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a>, and sometimes <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> must also be removed. 
</p><p>Natural gas is sometimes informally referred to simply as "gas", especially when it is being compared to other energy sources, such as oil or coal. However, it is not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a>, which is often shortened in colloquial usage to "gas", especially in North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Natural gas is measured in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_cubic_meter" title="Standard cubic meter">standard cubic meters</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_cubic_feet" title="Standard cubic feet">standard cubic feet</a>. The density compared to air ranges from 0.58 (16.8 g/mole, 0.71 kg per standard cubic meter) to as high as 0.79 (22.9 g/mole, 0.97 kg per scm), but generally less than 0.64 (18.5 g/mole, 0.78 kg per scm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> For comparison, pure methane (16.0425 g/mole) has a density 0.5539 times that of air (0.678 kg per standard cubic meter).
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Natural gas can come out of the ground and cause a long-burning fire. In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a>, the gas flames at <a href="/wiki/Mount_Chimaera" title="Mount Chimaera">Mount Chimaera</a> contributed to the legend of the fire-breathing creature <a href="/wiki/Chimera_(mythology)" title="Chimera (mythology)">Chimera</a>. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_China" title="Ancient China">ancient China</a>, gas resulting from the drilling for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brine_(solution)" title="Brine (solution)">brines</a> was first used in about 500 BC (possibly even 1000 BC<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>). The Chinese transported gas seeping from the ground in crude pipelines of bamboo to where it was used to boil salt water to <a href="/wiki/Salt_in_Chinese_history" title="Salt in Chinese history">extract the salt</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Ziliujing_District" title="Ziliujing District">Ziliujing District</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>The discovery and identification of natural gas in the Americas happened in 1626. In 1821, William Hart successfully dug the first natural gas well at <a href="/wiki/Fredonia,_New_York" title="Fredonia, New York">Fredonia, New York</a>, United States, which led to the formation of the Fredonia Gas Light Company. The city of <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> created the first municipally owned natural gas distribution venture in 1836.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> By 2009, 66,000 km<sup>3</sup> (16,000 mi<sup>3</sup>) (or 8%) had been used out of the total 850,000 km<sup>3</sup> (200,000 mi<sup>3</sup>) of estimated remaining recoverable reserves of natural gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldenergyoutlook.org_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-worldenergyoutlook.org-21">[21]</a></sup> Based on an estimated 2015 world consumption rate of about 3,400 km<sup>3</sup> (815 mi<sup>3</sup>) of gas per year, the total estimated remaining economically recoverable reserves of natural gas would last 250 years at current consumption rates. An annual increase in usage of 2–3% could result in currently recoverable reserves lasting significantly less, perhaps as few as 80 to 100 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldenergyoutlook.org_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-worldenergyoutlook.org-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_natural_gas_fields" title="List of natural gas fields">List of natural gas fields</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_natural_gas_proven_reserves" title="List of countries by natural gas proven reserves">List of countries by natural gas proven reserves</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_natural_gas_production" title="List of countries by natural gas production">List of countries by natural gas production</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_gas">Natural gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Natural gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2816" data-file-width="1872" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg/170px-BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg/255px-BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg/340px-BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Natural gas <a href="/wiki/Drilling_rig" title="Drilling rig">drilling rig</a> in Texas, USA</div></div></div>
<p>In the 19th century, natural gas was primarily obtained as a by-product of <a href="/wiki/Oil_well" title="Oil well">producing oil</a>.  The small, light gas carbon chains came out of solution as the extracted fluids underwent pressure reduction from the <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_reservoir" title="Petroleum reservoir">reservoir</a> to the surface, similar to uncapping a soft drink bottle where the carbon dioxide <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Effervesce" title="Effervesce">effervesces</a>. The gas was often viewed as a by-product, a hazard, and a disposal problem in active oil fields.  The large volumes produced could not be utilized until relatively expensive <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">pipeline</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_storage" title="Natural gas storage">storage</a> facilities were constructed to deliver the gas to consumer markets.
</p><p>Until the early part of the 20th century, most natural gas associated with oil was either simply released or <a href="/wiki/Gas_flare" title="Gas flare">burned off</a> at oil fields. <a href="/wiki/Gas_venting" title="Gas venting">Gas venting</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Production_flaring" title="Production flaring">production flaring</a> are still practised in modern times, but efforts are ongoing around the world to retire them, and to replace them with other commercially viable and useful alternatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Unwanted gas (or <a href="/wiki/Stranded_gas_reserve" title="Stranded gas reserve">stranded gas</a> without a market) is often returned to the reservoir with 'injection' wells while awaiting a possible future market or to re-pressurize the formation, which can enhance oil extraction rates from other wells. In regions with a high natural gas demand (such as the US), <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">pipelines</a> are constructed when it is economically feasible to transport gas from a wellsite to an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/End_consumer" title="End consumer">end consumer</a>.
</p><p>In addition to transporting gas via pipelines for use in power generation, other end uses for natural gas include export as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LNG" title="LNG">liquefied natural gas</a> (LNG) or conversion of natural gas into other liquid products via <a href="/wiki/Gas_to_liquids" title="Gas to liquids">gas to liquids</a> (GTL) technologies. GTL technologies can convert natural gas into liquids products such as gasoline, diesel or jet fuel. A variety of GTL technologies have been developed, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fischer%E2%80%93Tropsch" title="Fischer–Tropsch">Fischer–Tropsch</a> (F–T), methanol to gasoline (MTG) and <a href="/wiki/Syngas_to_gasoline_plus" title="Syngas to gasoline plus">syngas to gasoline plus</a> (STG+). F–T produces a synthetic crude that can be further refined into finished products, while MTG can produce synthetic gasoline from natural gas. STG+ can produce drop-in gasoline, diesel, jet fuel and aromatic chemicals directly from natural gas via a single-loop process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-STG+_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-STG+-24">[24]</a></sup> In 2011, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Dutch_Shell" title="Royal Dutch Shell">Royal Dutch Shell's</a> 140,000 barrels (22,000 m<sup>3</sup>) per day F–T plant went into operation in <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Natural gas can be "associated" (found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_field" title="Oil field">oil fields</a>), or "non-associated" (isolated in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_gas_field" title="Natural gas field">natural gas fields</a>), and is also found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_bed" title="Coal bed">coal beds</a> (as <a href="/wiki/Coalbed_methane" title="Coalbed methane">coalbed methane</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> It sometimes contains a significant amount of <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Butane" title="Butane">butane</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pentane" title="Pentane">pentane</a>—heavier hydrocarbons removed for commercial use prior to the <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> being sold as a consumer fuel or chemical plant feedstock. Non-hydrocarbons such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> (rarely), and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> must also be removed before the natural gas can be transported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Natural gas extracted from oil wells is called casinghead gas (whether or not truly produced up the annulus and through a casinghead outlet) or associated gas. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_gas_industry" title="Natural gas industry">natural gas industry</a> is extracting an increasing quantity of gas from challenging <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_petroleum_industry_in_Canada_(natural_gas)#Unconventional_gas" title="History of the petroleum industry in Canada (natural gas)">resource types</a>: <a href="/wiki/Sour_gas" title="Sour gas">sour gas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tight_gas" title="Tight gas">tight gas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shale_gas" title="Shale gas">shale gas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coalbed_methane" title="Coalbed methane">coalbed methane</a>.
</p><p>There is some disagreement on which country has the largest proven gas reserves. Sources that consider that Russia has by far the largest proven reserves include the US CIA (47,600 km<sup>3</sup>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> the US Energy Information Administration (47,800 km<sup>3</sup>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> and OPEC (48,700 km<sup>3</sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> However, BP credits Russia with only 32,900 km<sup>3</sup>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> which would place it in second place, slightly behind Iran (33,100 to 33,800 km<sup>3</sup>, depending on the source). 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Countries_by_Natural_Gas_Proven_Reserves_(2014).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1398" data-file-width="2754" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Countries_by_Natural_Gas_Proven_Reserves_%282014%29.svg/440px-Countries_by_Natural_Gas_Proven_Reserves_%282014%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Countries_by_Natural_Gas_Proven_Reserves_%282014%29.svg/660px-Countries_by_Natural_Gas_Proven_Reserves_%282014%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Countries_by_Natural_Gas_Proven_Reserves_%282014%29.svg/880px-Countries_by_Natural_Gas_Proven_Reserves_%282014%29.svg.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Countries_by_Natural_Gas_Proven_Reserves_(2014).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Countries by <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_natural_gas_proven_reserves" title="List of countries by natural gas proven reserves">natural gas proven reserves</a> (2014), based on data from The World Factbook</div></div></div>
<p>It is estimated that there are about 900,000 km<sup>3</sup> of "unconventional" gas such as shale gas, of which 180,000 km<sup>3</sup> may be recoverable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> In turn, many studies from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIT" title="MIT">MIT</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_%26_Veatch" title="Black &amp; Veatch">Black &amp; Veatch</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">DOE</a> predict that natural gas will account for a larger portion of electricity generation and heat in the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>The world's largest gas field is the offshore <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Pars_/_North_Dome_Gas-Condensate_field" title="South Pars / North Dome Gas-Condensate field">South Pars / North Dome Gas-Condensate field</a>, shared between Iran and Qatar. It is estimated to have 51,000 cubic kilometers (12,000 cu mi) of natural gas and 50 billion barrels (7.9 billion cubic meters) of <a href="/wiki/Natural-gas_condensate" title="Natural-gas condensate">natural gas condensates</a>.
</p><p>Because natural gas is not a pure product, as the reservoir pressure drops when non-associated gas is extracted from a field under <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_fluid" title="Supercritical fluid">supercritical</a> (pressure/temperature) conditions, the higher molecular weight components may partially condense upon isothermic depressurizing—an effect called <a href="/wiki/Retrograde_condensation" title="Retrograde condensation">retrograde condensation</a>. The liquid thus formed may get trapped as the pores of the gas reservoir get depleted. One method to deal with this problem is to re-inject dried gas free of condensate to maintain the underground pressure and to allow re-evaporation and extraction of condensates. More frequently, the liquid condenses at the surface, and one of the tasks of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_gas_processing" title="Natural gas processing">gas plant</a> is to collect this condensate. The resulting liquid is called natural gas liquid (NGL) and has commercial value.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shale_gas">Shale gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Shale gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GasDepositDiagram.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="473" data-file-width="718" decoding="async" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/GasDepositDiagram.jpg/440px-GasDepositDiagram.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/GasDepositDiagram.jpg/660px-GasDepositDiagram.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bb/GasDepositDiagram.jpg 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GasDepositDiagram.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The location of <a href="/wiki/Shale_gas" title="Shale gas">shale gas</a> compared to other types of gas deposits</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shale_gas" title="Shale gas">Shale gas</a></div>
<p>Shale gas is natural gas produced from <a href="/wiki/Shale" title="Shale">shale</a>. Because shale has matrix permeability too low to allow gas to flow in economical quantities, shale gas wells depend on fractures to allow the gas to flow. Early shale gas wells depended on natural fractures through which gas flowed; almost all shale gas wells today require fractures artificially created by <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fracturing" title="Hydraulic fracturing">hydraulic fracturing</a>. Since 2000, shale gas has become a major source of natural gas in the United States and Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Because of increased shale gas production the United States was in 2014 the number one natural gas producer in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The production of shale gas in the United States has been described as a "shale gas revolution" and as "one of the landmark events in the 21st century."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the increased production in the United States, shale gas exploration is beginning in countries such as Poland, China, and South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Chinese geologists have identified the <a href="/wiki/Sichuan_Basin" title="Sichuan Basin">Sichuan Basin</a> as a promising target for shale gas drilling, because of the similarity of shales to those that have proven productive in the United States. Production from the Wei-201 well is between 10,000 and 20,000 m<sup>3</sup> per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> In late 2020, China National Petroleum Corporation claimed daily production of 20 million cubic meters of gas from its Changning-Weiyuan demonstration zone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (November 2021)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Town_gas">Town gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Town gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_manufactured_fuel_gases" title="History of manufactured fuel gases">History of manufactured fuel gases</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Town_gas" title="Town gas">Town gas</a> is a flammable gaseous fuel made by the destructive distillation of <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>. It contains a variety of calorific gases including <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, and other volatile <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>, together with small quantities of non-calorific gases such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, and is used in a similar way to natural gas. This is a historical technology and is not usually economically competitive with other sources of fuel gas today.
</p><p>Most town "gashouses" located in the eastern US in the late 19th and early 20th centuries were simple by-product <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a> ovens that heated bituminous coal in air-tight chambers. The gas driven off from the coal was collected and distributed through networks of pipes to residences and other buildings where it was used for cooking and lighting. (Gas heating did not come into widespread use until the last half of the 20th century.) The <a href="/wiki/Coal_tar" title="Coal tar">coal tar</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Asphalt" title="Asphalt">asphalt</a>) that collected in the bottoms of the gashouse ovens was often used for roofing and other waterproofing purposes, and when mixed with sand and gravel was used for paving streets.
</p>
<h3><span id="Crystallized_natural_gas_.E2.80.93_clathrates"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Crystallized_natural_gas_–_clathrates">Crystallized natural gas – clathrates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Crystallized natural gas – clathrates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Huge quantities of natural gas (primarily methane) exist in the form of <a href="/wiki/Methane_clathrate" title="Methane clathrate">clathrates</a> under sediment on offshore continental shelves and on land in arctic regions that experience <a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">permafrost</a>, such as those in <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>. Hydrates require a combination of high pressure and low temperature to form.
</p><p>In 2010, the cost of extracting natural gas from crystallized natural gas was estimated to be as much as twice the cost of extracting natural gas from conventional sources, and even higher from offshore deposits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2013, Japan Oil, Gas and Metals National Corporation (JOGMEC) announced that they had recovered commercially relevant quantities of natural gas from methane hydrate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Processing">Processing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Processing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_gas_processing" title="Natural gas processing">Natural gas processing</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aderklaa_-_Gasstation.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1717" data-file-width="3030" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Aderklaa_-_Gasstation.JPG/220px-Aderklaa_-_Gasstation.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Aderklaa_-_Gasstation.JPG/330px-Aderklaa_-_Gasstation.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Aderklaa_-_Gasstation.JPG/440px-Aderklaa_-_Gasstation.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aderklaa_-_Gasstation.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Natural gas processing plant in <a href="/wiki/Aderklaa" title="Aderklaa">Aderklaa</a>, Lower Austria</div></div></div>
<p>The image below is a schematic <a href="/wiki/Process_flow_diagram" title="Process flow diagram">block flow diagram</a> of a typical natural gas processing plant. It shows the various unit processes used to convert raw natural gas into sales gas pipelined to the end user markets.
</p><p>The block flow diagram also shows how processing of the raw natural gas yields byproduct sulfur, byproduct ethane, and natural gas liquids (NGL) propane, butanes and natural gasoline (denoted as <a href="/wiki/Pentanes" title="Pentanes">pentanes</a> +).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:746px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NatGasProcessing.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="524" data-file-width="744" decoding="async" height="524" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/NatGasProcessing.svg/744px-NatGasProcessing.svg.png" width="744"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption">Schematic flow diagram of a typical natural gas processing plant</div></div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demand">Demand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Demand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Gas_depletion" title="Gas depletion">Gas depletion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_-_Natural_Gas_Production_of_Countries.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="625" data-file-width="1425" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/World_-_Natural_Gas_Production_of_Countries.png/440px-World_-_Natural_Gas_Production_of_Countries.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/World_-_Natural_Gas_Production_of_Countries.png/660px-World_-_Natural_Gas_Production_of_Countries.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/World_-_Natural_Gas_Production_of_Countries.png/880px-World_-_Natural_Gas_Production_of_Countries.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_-_Natural_Gas_Production_of_Countries.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_natural_gas_production" title="List of countries by natural gas production">Natural gas extraction by countries</a> in cubic meters per year around 2013</div></div></div><p>As of mid-2020, natural gas production in the US had peaked three times, with current levels exceeding both previous peaks. It reached 24.1 trillion cubic feet per year in 1973, followed by a decline, and reached 24.5 trillion cubic feet in 2001. After a brief drop, withdrawals increased nearly every year since 2006 (owing to the <a href="/wiki/Shale_gas_in_the_United_States#Barnett_and_beyond" title="Shale gas in the United States">shale gas boom</a>), with 2017 production at 33.4 trillion cubic feet and 2019 production at 40.7 trillion cubic feet. After the third peak in December 2019, extraction continued to fall from March onward due to decreased demand caused by the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic_in_the_United_States" title="COVID-19 pandemic in the United States">COVID-19 pandemic in the US</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EIAThirdPeak_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-EIAThirdPeak-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2021_global_energy_crisis" title="2021 global energy crisis">2021 global energy crisis</a> was driven by a global surge in demand as the world quit the economic recession caused by COVID-19, particularly due to strong energy demand in Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Storage_and_transport">Storage and transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Storage and transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_natural_gas_pipelines" title="List of natural gas pipelines">List of natural gas pipelines</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polyethylene_gas_main.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1166" data-file-width="844" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Polyethylene_gas_main.jpg/170px-Polyethylene_gas_main.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Polyethylene_gas_main.jpg/255px-Polyethylene_gas_main.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Polyethylene_gas_main.jpg/340px-Polyethylene_gas_main.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polyethylene_gas_main.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Polyethylene" title="Polyethylene">Polyethylene</a> plastic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_main" title="Gas main">main</a> being placed in a <a href="/wiki/Trench" title="Trench">trench</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gas_underpipe_warning_notice.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1238" signs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></div></div></div><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-Update plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" <p>Because of its low density, it is not easy to store natural gas or to transport it by vehicle. Natural gas <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">pipelines</a> are impractical across oceans, since the gas needs to be cooled down and compressed, as the friction in the pipeline causes the gas to heat up. Many <a href="/wiki/List_of_natural_gas_pipelines#North_America" title="List of natural gas pipelines">existing pipelines in the United States</a> are close to reaching their capacity, prompting some politicians representing northern states to speak of potential shortages. The large trade cost implies that natural gas markets are globally much less integrated, causing significant price differences across countries. In <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>, the gas pipeline network is already dense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (November 2017)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed. (November 2017)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> New pipelines are planned or under construction in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a> and between gas fields in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Africa" title="Northern Africa">Northern Africa</a> and Western Europe.
</p><p>Whenever gas is bought or sold at custody transfer points, rules and agreements are made regarding the gas quality. These may include the maximum allowable concentration of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide"><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S</span></a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor"><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span></a>. Usually sales quality gas that has been <a href="/wiki/Natural-gas_processing" title="Natural-gas processing">treated to remove contamination</a> is traded on a "dry gas" basis and is required to be commercially free from objectionable odours, materials, and dust or other solid or liquid matter, waxes, gums and gum forming constituents, which might damage or adversely affect operation of equipment downstream of the custody transfer point.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/LNG_carrier" title="LNG carrier">LNG carriers</a> transport <a href="/wiki/Liquefied_natural_gas" title="Liquefied natural gas">liquefied natural gas</a> (LNG) across oceans, while <a href="/wiki/Tank_truck" title="Tank truck">tank trucks</a> can carry liquefied or compressed natural gas (CNG) over shorter distances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Sea transport using <a href="/wiki/CNG_carrier" title="CNG carrier">CNG carrier</a> ships that are now under development may be competitive with LNG transport in specific conditions.
</p><p>Gas is turned into liquid at a <a href="/wiki/Liquefaction_of_gases" title="Liquefaction of gases">liquefaction</a> plant, and is returned to gas form at <a href="/wiki/Gasification" title="Gasification">regasification</a> plant at the <a href="/wiki/Liquefied_natural_gas_terminal" title="Liquefied natural gas terminal">terminal</a>. Shipborne regasification equipment is also used. LNG is the preferred form for long distance, high volume transportation of natural gas, whereas pipeline is preferred for transport for distances up to 4,000 km (2,500 mi) over land and approximately half that distance offshore.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CNG" title="CNG">CNG</a> is transported at high pressure, typically above 200 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">bars</a> (20,000 kPa; 2,900 psi). Compressors and decompression equipment are less capital intensive and may be economical in smaller unit sizes than liquefaction/regasification plants. Natural gas trucks and carriers may transport natural gas directly to end-users, or to distribution points such as pipelines.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Manlove_gas_storage_facility_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1506" data-file-width="2363" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Manlove_gas_storage_facility_crop.jpg/220px-Manlove_gas_storage_facility_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Manlove_gas_storage_facility_crop.jpg/330px-Manlove_gas_storage_facility_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Manlove_gas_storage_facility_crop.jpg/440px-Manlove_gas_storage_facility_crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Manlove_gas_storage_facility_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peoples_Gas" title="Peoples Gas">Peoples Gas</a> Manlove Field <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_storage" title="Natural gas storage">natural gas storage</a> area in <a href="/wiki/Newcomb_Township,_Champaign_County,_Illinois" title="Newcomb Township, Champaign County, Illinois">Newcomb Township, Champaign County, Illinois</a>. In the foreground (left) is one of the numerous wells for the underground storage area, with an LNG plant, and above ground storage tanks are in the background (right).</div></div></div>
<p>In the past, the natural gas which was recovered in the course of recovering <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> could not be profitably sold, and was simply burned at the oil field in a process known as <a href="/wiki/Gas_flare" title="Gas flare">flaring</a>. Flaring is now illegal in many countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Additionally, higher demand in the last 20–30 years has made production of gas associated with oil economically viable. As a further option, the gas is now sometimes re-<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/inject" title="wikt:inject">injected</a> into the formation for <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_oil_recovery" title="Enhanced oil recovery">enhanced oil recovery</a> by pressure maintenance as well as miscible or immiscible flooding. Conservation, re-injection, or flaring of natural gas associated with oil is primarily dependent on proximity to markets (pipelines), and regulatory restrictions.
</p><p>Natural gas can be indirectly exported through the absorption in other physical output. A recent study suggests that the expansion of shale gas production in the US has caused prices to drop relative to other countries. This has caused a boom in energy intensive manufacturing sector exports, whereby the average dollar unit of US manufacturing exports has almost tripled its energy content between 1996 and 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>A "master gas system" was invented in <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> in the late 1970s, ending any necessity for flaring. Satellite and nearby infra-red camera observations, however, shows that flaring<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> and venting<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> are still happening in some countries.
</p><p>Natural gas is used to generate electricity and heat for <a href="/wiki/Desalination" title="Desalination">desalination</a>. Similarly, some landfills that also discharge methane gases have been set up to capture the methane and generate electricity.
</p><p>Natural gas is often stored underground inside depleted gas reservoirs from previous gas wells, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salt_domes" title="Salt domes">salt domes</a>, or in tanks as liquefied natural gas.  The gas is injected in a time of low demand and extracted when demand picks up. Storage nearby end users helps to meet volatile demands, but such storage may not always be practicable.
</p><p>With 15 countries accounting for 84% of the worldwide extraction, access to natural gas has become an important issue in international politics, and countries vie for control of pipelines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> In the first decade of the 21st century, <a href="/wiki/Gazprom" title="Gazprom">Gazprom</a>, the state-owned energy company in Russia, engaged in disputes with <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a> over the price of natural gas, which have created concerns that gas deliveries to parts of Europe could be cut off for political reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> The United States is preparing to export natural gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Floating_liquefied_natural_gas">Floating liquefied natural gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Floating liquefied natural gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Floating_liquefied_natural_gas" title="Floating liquefied natural gas">Floating liquefied natural gas</a> (FLNG) is an innovative technology designed to enable the development of offshore gas resources that would otherwise remain untapped due to environmental or economic factors which currently make them impractical to develop via a land-based LNG operation. FLNG technology also provides a number of environmental and economic advantages:
</p>
<ul><li>Environmental – Because all processing is done at the gas field, there is no requirement for long pipelines to shore, compression units to pump the gas to shore, dredging and jetty construction, and onshore construction of an LNG processing plant, which significantly reduces the environmental footprint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Avoiding construction also helps preserve marine and coastal environments. In addition, environmental disturbance will be minimised during decommissioning because the facility can easily be disconnected and removed before being refurbished and re-deployed elsewhere.</li>
<li>Economic – Where pumping gas to shore can be prohibitively expensive, FLNG makes development economically viable. As a result, it will open up new business opportunities for countries to develop offshore gas fields that would otherwise remain stranded, such as those offshore East Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Many gas and oil companies are considering the economic and environmental benefits of floating liquefied natural gas (FLNG). There are currently projects underway to construct five FLNG facilities. <a href="/wiki/Petronas" title="Petronas">Petronas</a> is close to completion on their FLNG-1<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daewoo_Shipbuilding_and_Marine_Engineering" title="Daewoo Shipbuilding and Marine Engineering">Daewoo Shipbuilding and Marine Engineering</a> and are underway on their FLNG-2 project<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> at <a href="/wiki/Samsung_Heavy_Industries" title="Samsung Heavy Industries">Samsung Heavy Industries</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shell_Prelude" title="Shell Prelude">Shell Prelude</a> is due to start production 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Browse_LNG" title="Browse LNG">Browse LNG</a> project will commence <a href="/wiki/Front-end_loading" title="Front-end loading">FEED</a> in 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Natural gas is primarily used in the northern hemisphere. North America and Europe are major consumers.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mid-stream_natural_gas">Mid-stream natural gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Mid-stream natural gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Often well head gases require removal of various hydrocarbon molecules contained within the gas. Some of these gases include <a href="/wiki/Heptane" title="Heptane">heptane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pentane" title="Pentane">pentane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a> and other hydrocarbons with molecular weights above <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>). The natural gas transmission lines extend to the natural gas processing plant or unit which removes the higher molecular weighted hydrocarbons to produce natural gas with energy content between 35–39 megajoules per cubic metre (950–1,050 British thermal units per cubic foot). The processed natural gas may then be used for residential, commercial and industrial uses.
</p><p>Natural gas flowing in the distribution lines is called mid-stream natural gas and is often used to power engines which rotate compressors. These compressors are required in the transmission line to pressurize and repressurize the mid-stream natural gas as the gas travels. Typically, natural gas powered engines require 35–39 MJ/m<sup>3</sup> (950–1,050 BTU/cu ft) natural gas to operate at the rotational name plate specifications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-american-environmental_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-american-environmental-70">[70]</a></sup> Several methods are used to remove these higher molecular weighted gases for use by the natural gas engine. A few technologies are as follows:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Joule%E2%80%93Thomson_effect" title="Joule–Thomson effect">Joule–Thomson skid</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryogenic" title="Cryogenic">Cryogenic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Chiller" title="Chiller">chiller</a> system</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical" title="Chemical">Chemical</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enzymology" title="Enzymology">enzymology</a> system<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-american-environmental_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-american-environmental-70">[70]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_generation">Power generation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Power generation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Gas-fired_power_plant" title="Gas-fired power plant">Gas-fired power plant</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Gas-fired_power_plant&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Berlin-mitte_heizkraftwerk-mitte_20060605_629.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1728" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Berlin-mitte_heizkraftwerk-mitte_20060605_629.jpg/220px-Berlin-mitte_heizkraftwerk-mitte_20060605_629.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Berlin-mitte_heizkraftwerk-mitte_20060605_629.jpg/330px-Berlin-mitte_heizkraftwerk-mitte_20060605_629.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Berlin-mitte_heizkraftwerk-mitte_20060605_629.jpg/440px-Berlin-mitte_heizkraftwerk-mitte_20060605_629.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Berlin-mitte_heizkraftwerk-mitte_20060605_629.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Cogeneration" title="Cogeneration">cogeneration</a> plant in <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Share-electricity-gas.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Share-electricity-gas.svg/220px-Share-electricity-gas.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Share-electricity-gas.svg/330px-Share-electricity-gas.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Share-electricity-gas.svg/440px-Share-electricity-gas.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Share-electricity-gas.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Share of electricity production from gas</div></div></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Gas-fired_power_plant" title="Gas-fired power plant">gas-fired power plant</a> or gas-fired power station or natural gas power plant is a <a href="/wiki/Thermal_power_station" title="Thermal power station">thermal power station</a> which burns natural gas to <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">generate electricity</a>. <a href="/wiki/List_of_natural_gas_power_stations" title="List of natural gas power stations">Natural gas power stations</a> generate almost a quarter of <a href="/wiki/Electric_energy_consumption" title="Electric energy consumption">world electricity</a> and a significant part of global <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas emissions">greenhouse gas emissions</a> and thus <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> However they can provide seasonal <a href="/wiki/Dispatchable_generation" title="Dispatchable generation">dispatchable generation</a> to balance <a href="/wiki/Variable_renewable_energy" title="Variable renewable energy">variable renewable energy</a> where <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">hydropower</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_interconnector" title="Electrical interconnector">interconnectors</a> are not available.</div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_use">Domestic use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Domestic use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GAS_GAS_manhole_cover_London.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4032" data-file-width="3024" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/GAS_GAS_manhole_cover_London.jpg/220px-GAS_GAS_manhole_cover_London.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/GAS_GAS_manhole_cover_London.jpg/330px-GAS_GAS_manhole_cover_London.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/GAS_GAS_manhole_cover_London.jpg/440px-GAS_GAS_manhole_cover_London.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GAS_GAS_manhole_cover_London.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Manhole for domestic gas supply, London, UK</div></div></div>
<p>Natural gas dispensed in a residential setting can generate temperatures in excess of 1,100 °C (2,000 °F) making it a powerful domestic cooking and heating fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> In much of the developed world it is supplied through pipes to homes, where it is used for many purposes including ranges and ovens, gas-heated <a href="/wiki/Clothes_dryer" title="Clothes dryer">clothes dryers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HVAC" title="HVAC">heating</a>/<a href="/wiki/Air_conditioning" title="Air conditioning">cooling</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Central_heating" title="Central heating">central heating</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AtoZ_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-AtoZ-73">[73]</a></sup> Heaters in homes and other buildings may include boilers, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Furnace_(house_heating)" title="Furnace (house heating)">furnaces</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Water_heating" title="Water heating">water heaters</a>. Both North America and Europe are major consumers of natural gas.
</p><p>Domestic appliances, furnaces, and boilers use low pressure, usually with a standard preassure around 1.7 kilopascals (0.25 psi) over atmospheric preassure. The pressures in the supply lines vary, either the standard utilization pressure (UP) mentioned above or elevated pressure (EP), which may be anywhere from 7 to 800 kilopascals (1 to 120 psi) over atmospheric preassure. Systems using EP have a <a href="/wiki/Pressure_regulator" title="Pressure regulator">regulator</a> at the service entrance to step down the pressure to UP. 
</p><p>Natural Gas Piping Systems inside buildings are often designed with pressures of 14 to 34 kilopascals (2 to 5 psi), and have downstream pressure regulators to reduce pressure as needed. The maximum allowable operating pressure for natural gas piping systems within a building is based on NFPA 54: National Fuel Gas Code, except when approved by the Public Safety Authority or when insurance companies have more stringent requirements. 
</p><p>Generally, natural gas system pressures are not allowed to exceed 5 psig (34.5 kPa) unless all of the following conditions are met:
</p>
<ul><li>The AHJ will allow a higher pressure.</li>
<li>The distribution pipe is welded. (Note: 2. Some jurisdictions may also require that welded joints be radiographed to verify continuity).</li>
<li>The pipes are closed for protection and placed in a ventilated area that does not allow gas accumulation.</li>
<li>The pipe is installed in the areas used for industrial processes, research, storage or mechanical equipment rooms.</li></ul>
<p>Generally, a maximum liquefied petroleum gas pressure of 20 psig (138 kPa) is allowed, provided the building is used specifically for industrial or research purposes and is constructed in accordance with NFPA 58: Liquefied Petroleum Gas Code, Chapter 7.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>A seismic earthquake valve operating at a pressure of 55 psig (3.7 bar) can stop the flow of natural gas into the site wide natural gas distribution piping network (that runs (outdoors underground, above building roofs, and or within the upper supports of a canopy roof). Seismic earthquake valves are designed for use at a maximum of 60 psig.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Australia, natural gas is transported from gas processing facilities to regulator stations via Transmission pipelines. Gas is then regulated down to distributed pressures and the gas is distributed around a gas network via gas mains. Small branches from the network, called services, connect individual domestic dwellings, or multi-dwelling buildings to the network. The networks typically range in pressures from 7 kPa (low pressure) to 515 kPa (high pressure). Gas is then regulated down to 1.1 kPa or 2.75 kPa, before being metered and passed to the consumer for domestic use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Natural gas mains are made from a variety of materials: historically cast iron, though more modern mains are made from steel or polyethylene.
</p><p>In the US <a href="/wiki/Compressed_natural_gas" title="Compressed natural gas">compressed natural gas</a> (CNG) is available in some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rural" title="Rural">rural</a> areas as an alternative to less expensive and more abundant LPG (<a href="/wiki/Liquefied_petroleum_gas" title="Liquefied petroleum gas">liquefied petroleum gas</a>), the dominant source of rural gas.  It is used in homes lacking direct connections to <a href="/wiki/Public_utility" title="Public utility">public utility</a> provided gas, or to fuel portable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grill_(cooking)" title="Grill (cooking)">grills</a>.  Natural gas is also supplied by independent natural gas suppliers through <a href="/wiki/Natural_Gas_Choice" title="Natural Gas Choice">Natural Gas Choice</a> programs<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (May 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> throughout the United States.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WMATA_3006.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/WMATA_3006.jpg/220px-WMATA_3006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/WMATA_3006.jpg/330px-WMATA_3006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/WMATA_3006.jpg/440px-WMATA_3006.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WMATA_3006.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a> <a href="/wiki/Metrobus_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Metrobus (Washington, D.C.)">Metrobus</a>, which runs on natural gas</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Transportation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>CNG is a cleaner and also cheaper alternative to other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile">automobile</a> fuels such as <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> (petrol).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> By the end of 2014, there were over 20 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_gas_vehicles" title="Natural gas vehicles">natural gas vehicles</a> worldwide, led by <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> (3.5 million), <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> (3.3 million), <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> (2.8 million), <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a> (2.5 million), <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> (1.8 million), and <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> (1.8 million).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> The energy efficiency is generally equal to that of gasoline engines, but lower compared with modern diesel engines. Gasoline/petrol vehicles converted to run on natural gas suffer because of the low <a href="/wiki/Compression_ratio" title="Compression ratio">compression ratio</a> of their engines, resulting in a cropping of delivered power while running on natural gas (10–15%). CNG-specific engines, however, use a higher compression ratio due to this fuel's higher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Octane_number" title="Octane number">octane number</a> of 120–130.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Besides use in road vehicles, CNG can also be used in aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Compressed natural gas has been used in some aircraft like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aviat_Aircraft" title="Aviat Aircraft">Aviat Aircraft</a> Husky 200 CNG<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> and the Chromarat VX-1 KittyHawk<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>LNG is also being used in aircraft. <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russian</a> aircraft manufacturer <a href="/wiki/Tupolev" title="Tupolev">Tupolev</a> for instance is running a development program to produce LNG- and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>-powered aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> The program has been running since the mid-1970s, and seeks to develop LNG and hydrogen variants of the <a href="/wiki/Tupolev_Tu-204" title="Tupolev Tu-204">Tu-204</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tupolev_Tu-334" title="Tupolev Tu-334">Tu-334</a> passenger aircraft, and also the <a href="/wiki/Tupolev_Tu-330" title="Tupolev Tu-330">Tu-330</a> cargo aircraft. Depending on the current market price for jet fuel and LNG, fuel for an LNG-powered aircraft could cost 5,000 <a href="/wiki/Russian_ruble" title="Russian ruble">rubles</a> (US$100) less per tonne, roughly 60%, with considerable reductions to <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxide" title="Nitrogen oxide">nitrogen oxide</a> emissions.
</p><p>The advantages of liquid methane as a jet engine fuel are that it has more specific energy than the standard <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a> mixes do and that its low temperature can help cool the air which the engine compresses for greater volumetric efficiency, in effect replacing an <a href="/wiki/Intercooler" title="Intercooler">intercooler</a>. Alternatively, it can be used to lower the temperature of the exhaust.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fertilizers">Fertilizers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Fertilizers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/2007%E2%80%932008_world_food_price_crisis" title="2007–2008 world food price crisis">2007–2008 world food price crisis</a></div>
<p>Natural gas is a major feedstock for the production of <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, via the <a href="/wiki/Haber_process" title="Haber process">Haber process</a>, for use in <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizer</a> production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AtoZ_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-AtoZ-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> The development of synthetic nitrogen fertilizer has significantly supported global <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">population growth</a> — it has been estimated that almost half the people on the Earth are currently fed as a result of synthetic nitrogen fertilizer use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrogen">Hydrogen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Hydrogen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Industrial_gas" title="Industrial gas">Industrial gas</a></div>
<p>Natural gas can be used to produce <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, with one common method being the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_reformer" title="Hydrogen reformer">hydrogen reformer</a>. Hydrogen has many applications: it is a primary feedstock for the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_industry" title="Chemical industry">chemical industry</a>, a hydrogenating agent, an important commodity for oil refineries, and the fuel source in <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_vehicle" title="Hydrogen vehicle">hydrogen vehicles</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_and_fish_feed">Animal and fish feed</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Animal and fish feed">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Protein rich animal and fish feed is produced by feeding natural gas to <a href="/wiki/Methylococcus_capsulatus" title="Methylococcus capsulatus">Methylococcus capsulatus</a> bacteria on commercial scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Natural gas is also used in the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">fabrics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plastic" title="Plastic">plastics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paint" title="Paint">paint</a>, <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_oil" title="Synthetic oil">synthetic oil</a>, and other products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The first step in the valorization of natural gas components is usually the<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (January 2021)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> of the alkane into olefin. The oxidative dehydrogenation of ethane leads to ethylene which can be converted forther to ethylene epoxide, ethylene glycol, acetaldehyde<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> or other olefins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Propane can be converted to propylene<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> or can be oxidized to acrylic acid<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> and acrylonitrile.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_effects">Environmental effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Environmental effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
