<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_the_energy_industry" title="Environmental impact of the energy industry">Environmental impact of the energy industry</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greenhouse_gas_effect_of_natural_gas_release">Greenhouse gas effect of natural gas release</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Greenhouse gas effect of natural gas release">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_methane" title="Atmospheric methane">Atmospheric methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gas_venting" title="Gas venting">Gas venting</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fugitive_gas_emissions" title="Fugitive gas emissions">Fugitive gas emissions</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1979-_Radiative_forcing_-_climate_change_-_global_warming_-_EPA_NOAA.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/1979-_Radiative_forcing_-_climate_change_-_global_warming_-_EPA_NOAA.svg/330px-1979-_Radiative_forcing_-_climate_change_-_global_warming_-_EPA_NOAA.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/1979-_Radiative_forcing_-_climate_change_-_global_warming_-_EPA_NOAA.svg/495px-1979-_Radiative_forcing_-_climate_change_-_global_warming_-_EPA_NOAA.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/1979-_Radiative_forcing_-_climate_change_-_global_warming_-_EPA_NOAA.svg/660px-1979-_Radiative_forcing_-_climate_change_-_global_warming_-_EPA_NOAA.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1979-_Radiative_forcing_-_climate_change_-_global_warming_-_EPA_NOAA.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The warming influence (called <a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">radiative forcing</a>) of long-lived greenhouse gases has nearly doubled in 40 years, with carbon dioxide and methane being the dominant drivers of global warming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA_NOAA_ClimateForcing1979_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPA_NOAA_ClimateForcing1979-99">[99]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Human activity is responsible for about 60% of all <a href="/wiki/Methane_emissions" title="Methane emissions">methane emissions</a> and for most of the resulting increase in atmospheric methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nationalgeo_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-nationalgeo-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_AR5_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_AR5-102">[102]</a></sup> Natural gas is intentionally released or is otherwise known to leak during the extraction, storage, transportation, and distribution of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a>.  Globally, methane accounts for an estimated 33% of anthropogenic <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> warming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Initiative_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Initiative-103">[103]</a></sup> The decomposition of municipal solid waste (a source of <a href="/wiki/Landfill_gas" title="Landfill gas">landfill gas</a>) and wastewater account for an additional 18% of such emissions.   These estimates include substantial uncertainties<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> which should be reduced in the near future with improved <a href="/wiki/Earth_observation_satellite" title="Earth observation satellite">satellite</a> measurements, such as those planned for <a href="/wiki/MethaneSAT" title="MethaneSAT">MethaneSAT</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AGI_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-AGI-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>After release to the atmosphere, methane is removed by gradual oxidation to carbon dioxide and water by hydroxyl radicals (<span class="chemf nowrap">OH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>) formed in the troposphere or stratosphere, giving the overall chemical reaction <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + 2<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + 2<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manahan_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Manahan-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_GISS_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_GISS-106">[106]</a></sup> While the lifetime of atmospheric methane is relatively short when compared to carbon dioxide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ny_times_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ny_times-107">[107]</a></sup> with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of about 7 years, it is more efficient at trapping heat in the atmosphere, so that a given quantity of methane has 84 times the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global-warming_potential" title="Global-warming potential">global-warming potential</a> of carbon dioxide over a 20-year period and 28 times over a 100-year period. Natural gas is thus a potent greenhouse gas due to the strong <a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">radiative forcing</a> of methane in the short term, and the continuing effects of carbon dioxide in the longer term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_AR5_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_AR5-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>Targeted efforts to reduce warming quickly by reducing anthropogenic methane emissions is a <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation" title="Climate change mitigation">climate change mitigation</a> strategy supported by the <a href="/wiki/Global_Methane_Initiative" title="Global Methane Initiative">Global Methane Initiative</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Initiative_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-Initiative-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greenhouse_gas_emissions">Greenhouse gas emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Greenhouse gas emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When refined and burned, natural gas can produce 25–30% less carbon dioxide per <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">joule</a> delivered than oil, and 40–45% less than coal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NGandE_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-NGandE-108">[108]</a></sup> It can also produce potentially fewer toxic <a href="/wiki/Pollutant" title="Pollutant">pollutants</a> than other hydrocarbon fuels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NGandE_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-NGandE-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NGinASIA_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-NGinASIA-109">[109]</a></sup> However, compared to other major fossil fuels, natural gas causes more emissions in relative terms during the production and transportation of the fuel, meaning that the life cycle greenhouse gas emissions are about 50% higher than the direct emissions from the site of consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>In terms of the warming effect over 100 years, natural gas production and use comprises about one fifth of human <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas emissions">greenhouse gas emissions</a>, and this contribution is growing rapidly.  Globally, natural gas use emitted about 7.8 billion tons of <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> in 2020 (including flaring), while coal and oil use emitted 14.4 and 12 billion tons, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> The IEA estimates the energy sector (oil, natural gas, coal and bioenergy) to be responsible for about 40% of human methane emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>  According to the <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Sixth_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Sixth Assessment Report">IPCC Sixth Assessment Report</a>, natural gas consumption grew by 15% between 2015 and 2019, compared to a 5% increase in oil and oil product consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>The continued financing and construction of new gas <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">pipelines</a> indicates that huge emissions of fossil greenhouse gases could be locked-in for 40 to 50 years into the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GEM2019_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-GEM2019-115">[115]</a></sup> In the U.S. state of <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> alone, five new long-distance gas pipelines have been under construction, with the first entering service in 2019,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> and the others scheduled to come online during 2020–2022.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DOE-GFV-2019_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-DOE-GFV-2019-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 23">: 23 </span></sup>
</p><p>To reduce its greenhouse emissions, the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> is subsidizing a transition away from natural gas for all homes in the country by 2050. In <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>, no new residential gas accounts have been allowed since 2018, and all homes in the city are expected to be converted by 2040 to use the excess heat from adjacent industrial buildings and operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
Some cities in the United States have started prohibiting gas hookups for new houses, with state laws passed and under consideration to either require electrification or prohibit local requirements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> The UK government is also experimenting with alternative home heating technologies to meet its climate goals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> To preserve their businesses, natural gas utilities in the United States have been lobbying for laws preventing local electrification ordinances, and are promoting <a href="/wiki/Renewable_natural_gas" title="Renewable natural gas">renewable natural gas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fuel" title="Hydrogen fuel">hydrogen fuel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_pollutants">Other pollutants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Other pollutants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Natural gas produces far lower amounts of <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/NOx" title="NOx">nitrogen oxides</a> than other fossil fuels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NGinASIA_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-NGinASIA-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radionuclides">Radionuclides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Radionuclides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Natural gas extraction also produces radioactive isotopes of <a href="/wiki/Polonium" title="Polonium">polonium</a> (Po-210), <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> (Pb-210) and <a href="/wiki/Radon" title="Radon">radon</a> (Rn-220). Radon is a gas with initial activity from 5 to 200,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Becquerels" title="Becquerels">becquerels</a> per cubic meter of gas. It decays rapidly to Pb-210 which can build up as a thin film in gas extraction equipment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety_concerns">Safety concerns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Safety concerns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gas_pipeline_odourant_injection_facility.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Gas_pipeline_odourant_injection_facility.JPG/220px-Gas_pipeline_odourant_injection_facility.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Gas_pipeline_odourant_injection_facility.JPG/330px-Gas_pipeline_odourant_injection_facility.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Gas_pipeline_odourant_injection_facility.JPG/440px-Gas_pipeline_odourant_injection_facility.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gas_pipeline_odourant_injection_facility.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pipeline odorant injection station</div></div></div><p>The natural gas extraction workforce face unique health and safety challenges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some gas fields yield <a href="/wiki/Sour_gas" title="Sour gas">sour gas</a> containing <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S</span>), a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toxic" title="Toxic">toxic</a> compound when inhaled. <a href="/wiki/Amine_gas_treating" title="Amine gas treating">Amine gas treating</a>, an industrial scale process which removes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acidic" title="Acidic">acidic</a> <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gaseous</a> components, is often used to remove hydrogen sulfide from natural gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Processing_Natural_Gas_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Processing_Natural_Gas-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>Extraction of natural gas (or oil) leads to decrease in pressure in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_reservoir" title="Oil reservoir">reservoir</a>. Such decrease in pressure in turn may result in <a href="/wiki/Subsidence" title="Subsidence">subsidence</a>, sinking of the ground above. Subsidence may affect ecosystems, waterways, sewer and water supply systems, foundations, and so on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fracking">Fracking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Fracking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_hydraulic_fracturing" title="Environmental impact of hydraulic fracturing">Environmental impact of hydraulic fracturing</a></div>
<p>Releasing natural gas from subsurface porous rock formations may be accomplished by a process called <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fracturing" title="Hydraulic fracturing">hydraulic fracturing</a> or "fracking". Since the first commercial hydraulic fracturing operation in 1949, approximately one million wells have been hydraulically fractured in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> The production of natural gas from hydraulically fractured wells has utilized the technological developments of directional and horizontal drilling, which improved access to natural gas in tight rock formations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Strong growth in the production of unconventional gas from hydraulically fractured wells occurred between 2000 and 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>In hydraulic fracturing, well operators force water mixed with a variety of chemicals through the wellbore casing into the rock. The high pressure water breaks up or "fracks" the rock, which releases gas from the rock formation. Sand and other particles are added to the water as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proppant" title="Proppant">proppant</a> to keep the fractures in the rock open, thus enabling the gas to flow into the casing and then to the surface. Chemicals are added to the fluid to perform such functions as reducing friction and inhibiting corrosion. After the "frack", oil or gas is extracted and 30–70% of the frack fluid, i.e. the mixture of water, chemicals, sand, etc., flows back to the surface. Many gas-bearing formations also contain water, which will flow up the wellbore to the surface along with the gas, in both hydraulically fractured and non-hydraulically fractured wells. This <a href="/wiki/Produced_water" title="Produced water">produced water</a> often has a high content of salt and other dissolved minerals that occur in the formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>The volume of water used to hydraulically fracture wells varies according to the hydraulic fracturing technique.  In the United States, the average volume of water used per hydraulic fracture has been reported as nearly 7,375 gallons for vertical oil and gas wells prior to 1953, nearly 197,000 gallons for vertical oil and gas wells between 2000 and 2010, and nearly 3 million gallons for horizontal gas wells between 2000 and 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>Determining which fracking technique is appropriate for well productivity depends largely on the properties of the reservoir rock from which to extract oil or gas.  If the rock is characterized by low-permeability – which refers to its ability to let substances, i.e. gas, pass through it, then the rock may be considered a source of <a href="/wiki/Tight_gas" title="Tight gas">tight gas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Fracking for shale gas, which is currently also known as a source of <a href="/wiki/Unconventional_gas" title="Unconventional gas">unconventional gas</a>, involves drilling a borehole vertically until it reaches a lateral shale rock formation, at which point the drill turns to follow the rock for hundreds or thousands of feet horizontally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> In contrast, conventional oil and gas sources are characterized by higher rock permeability, which naturally enables the flow of oil or gas into the wellbore with less intensive hydraulic fracturing techniques than the production of tight gas has required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> The decades in development of drilling technology for conventional and <a href="/wiki/Unconventional_oil" title="Unconventional oil">unconventional oil</a> and gas production have not only improved access to natural gas in low-permeability reservoir rocks, but also posed significant adverse impacts on environmental and public health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>The US EPA has acknowledged that toxic, carcinogenic chemicals, i.e. benzene and ethylbenzene, have been used as gelling agents in water and chemical mixtures for high volume horizontal fracturing (HVHF).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> Following the hydraulic fracture in HVHF, the water, chemicals, and frack fluid that return to the well's surface, called flowback or produced water, may contain radioactive materials, heavy metals, natural salts, and hydrocarbons which exist naturally in shale rock formations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> Fracking chemicals, radioactive materials, heavy metals, and salts that are removed from the HVHF well by well operators are so difficult to remove from the water they're mixed with, and would so heavily <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">pollute</a> the <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">water cycle</a>, that most of the flowback is either recycled into other fracking operations or injected into deep underground wells, eliminating the water that HVHF required from the hydrologic cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historically low gas prices have delayed the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_renaissance" title="Nuclear renaissance">nuclear renaissance</a>, as well as the development of <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_energy" title="Solar thermal energy">solar thermal energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Added_odor">Added odor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Added odor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Natural gas in its native state is colorless and almost <a href="/wiki/Odor" title="Odor">odorless</a>.  In order to assist consumers in detecting <a href="/wiki/Leak" title="Leak">leaks</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Odorizer" title="Odorizer">odorizer</a> with a scent similar to rotten eggs, <a href="/wiki/Tert-Butylthiol" title="Tert-Butylthiol">tert-Butylthiol</a> (t-butyl mercaptan), is added. Sometimes a related compound, <a href="/wiki/Tetrahydrothiophene" title="Tetrahydrothiophene">thiophane</a>, may be used in the mixture. Situations in which an odorant that is added to natural gas can be detected by analytical instrumentation, but cannot be properly detected by an observer with a normal sense of smell, have occurred in the natural gas industry.  This is caused by odor masking, when one odorant overpowers the sensation of another. As of 2011, the industry is conducting research on the causes of odor masking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Risk_of_explosion">Risk of explosion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Risk of explosion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fire_engines_in_Kiev,_Ukraine.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Fire_engines_in_Kiev%2C_Ukraine.JPG/220px-Fire_engines_in_Kiev%2C_Ukraine.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Fire_engines_in_Kiev%2C_Ukraine.JPG/330px-Fire_engines_in_Kiev%2C_Ukraine.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Fire_engines_in_Kiev%2C_Ukraine.JPG/440px-Fire_engines_in_Kiev%2C_Ukraine.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fire_engines_in_Kiev,_Ukraine.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gas network emergency vehicle responding to a major fire in <a href="/wiki/Kyiv" title="Kyiv">Kyiv</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/List_of_pipeline_accidents" title="List of pipeline accidents">Explosions caused by natural</a> <a href="/wiki/Gas_leak" title="Gas leak">gas leaks</a> occur a few times each year. Individual homes, small businesses and other structures are most frequently affected when an internal leak builds up gas inside the structure. Leaks often result from excavation work, such as when contractors dig and strike pipelines, sometimes without knowing any damage resulted. Frequently, the blast is powerful enough to significantly damage a building but leave it standing. In these cases, the people inside tend to have minor to moderate injuries. Occasionally, the gas can collect in high enough quantities to cause a deadly explosion, destroying one or more buildings in the process. Many building codes now forbid the installation of gas pipes inside cavity walls or below floor boards to mitigate against this risk. Gas usually dissipates readily outdoors, but can sometimes collect in dangerous quantities if <a href="/wiki/High_pressure_jet" title="High pressure jet">flow rates are high enough</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> However, considering the tens of millions of structures that use the fuel, the individual risk of using natural gas is low.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Risk_of_carbon_monoxide_inhalation">Risk of carbon monoxide inhalation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Risk of carbon monoxide inhalation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Natural gas heating systems may cause <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide_poisoning" title="Carbon monoxide poisoning">carbon monoxide poisoning</a> if unvented or poorly vented. Improvements in natural gas furnace designs have greatly reduced CO poisoning concerns. <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide_detector" title="Carbon monoxide detector">Detectors</a> are also available that warn of carbon monoxide or explosive gases such as methane and propane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Energy_content.2C_statistics.2C_and_pricing"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_content,_statistics,_and_pricing">Energy content, statistics, and pricing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Energy content, statistics, and pricing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_prices" title="Natural gas prices">Natural gas prices</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Billion_cubic_metres_of_natural_gas" title="Billion cubic metres of natural gas">Billion cubic metres of natural gas</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Henry_hub_NG_prices.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="391" data-file-width="517" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Henry_hub_NG_prices.svg/330px-Henry_hub_NG_prices.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Henry_hub_NG_prices.svg/495px-Henry_hub_NG_prices.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Henry_hub_NG_prices.svg/660px-Henry_hub_NG_prices.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Henry_hub_NG_prices.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_prices" title="Natural gas prices">Natural gas prices</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Henry_Hub" title="Henry Hub">Henry Hub</a> in US dollars per million BTUs</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Natural_Gas_Price_Comparison.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="338" data-file-width="566" decoding="async" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Natural_Gas_Price_Comparison.png/330px-Natural_Gas_Price_Comparison.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Natural_Gas_Price_Comparison.png/495px-Natural_Gas_Price_Comparison.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6c/Natural_Gas_Price_Comparison.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Natural_Gas_Price_Comparison.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison of natural gas prices in Japan, United Kingdom, and United States, 2007–2011</div></div></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Update plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="Ambox current red Americas.svg" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="360" decoding="async" height="34" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png 2x" width="42"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2022</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Quantities of natural gas are measured in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_cubic_meter" title="Standard cubic meter">standard cubic meters</a> (cubic meter of gas at temperature 15 °C (59 °F) and pressure 101.325 kPa (14.6959 psi)) or <a href="/wiki/Standard_cubic_foot" title="Standard cubic foot">standard cubic feet</a> (cubic foot of gas at temperature 60.0 °F and pressure 14.73 psi (101.6 kPa)), 1 standard cubic meter = 35.301 standard cubic feet. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Higher_heating_value" title="Higher heating value">gross heat of combustion</a> of commercial quality natural gas is around 39 MJ/m<sup>3</sup> (0.31 kWh/cu ft), but this can vary by several percent. This is about 50 to 54 MJ/kg depending on the density.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>Except in the European Union, the U.S., and Canada, natural gas is sold in gigajoule retail units. LNG (liquefied natural gas) and LPG (<a href="/wiki/Liquefied_petroleum_gas" title="Liquefied petroleum gas">liquefied petroleum gas</a>) are traded in metric tonnes (1,000 kg) or million BTU as spot deliveries. Long term natural gas distribution contracts are signed in cubic meters, and LNG contracts are in metric tonnes. The LNG and LPG is transported by specialized <a href="/wiki/LNG_carrier" title="LNG carrier">transport ships</a>, as the gas is liquified at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryogenic" title="Cryogenic">cryogenic</a> temperatures. The specification of each LNG/LPG cargo will usually contain the energy content, but this information is in general not available to the public.
</p><p>In the Russian Federation, <a href="/wiki/Gazprom" title="Gazprom">Gazprom</a> sold approximately 250 billion cubic meters (8.8 trillion cubic feet) of natural gas in 2008. In 2013 they produced 487.4 billion cubic meters (17.21 trillion cubic feet) of natural and associated gas. Gazprom supplied Europe with 161.5 billion cubic meters (5.70 trillion cubic feet) of gas in 2013. The European Union aims to cut its gas dependency on Russia by two-thirds in 2022.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 2015, possibly the largest natural gas discovery in history was made and notified by an Italian gas company ENI. The energy company indicated that it has unearthed a <a href="/wiki/Giant_oil_and_gas_fields" title="Giant oil and gas fields">"supergiant" gas field</a> in the Mediterranean Sea covering about 40 square miles (100 km<sup>2</sup>). This was named the <a href="/wiki/Zohr_Field" title="Zohr Field">Zohr</a> gas field and could hold a potential 30 trillion cubic feet (850 billion cubic meters) of natural gas. ENI said that the energy is about 5.5 billion barrels of oil equivalent [BOE] (3.4<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>10</sup> GJ). The <a href="/wiki/Zohr_Field" title="Zohr Field">Zohr</a> field was found in the deep waters off the northern coast of Egypt and ENI claims that it will be the largest ever in the Mediterranean and even the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Union">European Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: European Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Gas prices for end users vary greatly across the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">EU</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> A single European energy market, one of the key objectives of the EU, should level the prices of gas in all EU member states. Moreover, it would help to resolve supply and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> issues,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> as well as strengthen relations with other Mediterranean countries and foster investments in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a> has been asked by the US to supply emergency gas to the EU in case of supply disruptions in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2021%E2%80%932022_Russo-Ukrainian_crisis" title="2021–2022 Russo-Ukrainian crisis">Russo-Ukrainian crisis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Natural_Gas_Production.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="490" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/US_Natural_Gas_Production.svg/330px-US_Natural_Gas_Production.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/US_Natural_Gas_Production.svg/495px-US_Natural_Gas_Production.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/US_Natural_Gas_Production.svg/660px-US_Natural_Gas_Production.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_Natural_Gas_Production.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>US Natural Gas Marketed Production 1900 to 2012 (US EIA data)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gas_Production_Top_5_Countries.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1350" data-file-width="2025" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Gas_Production_Top_5_Countries.png/330px-Gas_Production_Top_5_Countries.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Gas_Production_Top_5_Countries.png/495px-Gas_Production_Top_5_Countries.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Gas_Production_Top_5_Countries.png/660px-Gas_Production_Top_5_Countries.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gas_Production_Top_5_Countries.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Trends in the top five natural gas-producing countries (US EIA data)</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/United_States_customary_units" title="United States customary units">US units</a>, one standard cubic foot (28 L) of natural gas produces around 1,028 <a href="/wiki/British_thermal_unit" title="British thermal unit">British thermal units</a> (1,085 kJ). The actual heating value when the water formed does not condense is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_heating_value" title="Lower heating value">net heat of combustion</a> and can be as much as 10% less.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, retail sales are often in units of <a href="/wiki/Therm" title="Therm">therms</a> (th); 1 therm = 100,000 BTU. Gas sales to domestic consumers are often in units of 100 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_cubic_feet" title="Standard cubic feet">standard cubic feet</a> (scf). <a href="/wiki/Gas_meter" title="Gas meter">Gas meters</a> measure the volume of gas used, and this is converted to therms by multiplying the volume by the energy content of the gas used during that period, which varies slightly over time. The typical annual consumption of a single family residence is 1,000 therms or one <a href="/wiki/Residential_Customer_Equivalent" title="Residential Customer Equivalent">Residential Customer Equivalent</a> (RCE). Wholesale transactions are generally done in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decatherm" title="Decatherm">decatherms</a> (Dth), thousand decatherms (MDth), or million decatherms (MMDth). A million decatherms is a trillion BTU, roughly a billion cubic feet of natural gas.
</p><p>The price of natural gas varies greatly depending on location and type of consumer. The typical caloric value of natural gas is roughly 1,000 BTU per cubic foot, depending on gas composition. Natural gas in the United States is traded as a <a href="/wiki/Futures_contract" title="Futures contract">futures contract</a> on the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Mercantile_Exchange" title="New York Mercantile Exchange">New York Mercantile Exchange</a>. Each contract is for 10,000 million BTU or 10 billion BTU (10,551 GJ). Thus, if the price of gas is $10/million BTU on the NYMEX, the contract is worth $100,000.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Canada uses <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_units" title="Metric units">metric</a> measure for internal trade of petrochemical products. Consequently, natural gas is sold by the gigajoule (GJ), cubic meter (m<sup>3</sup>) or thousand cubic meters (E3m3). Distribution infrastructure and meters almost always meter volume (cubic foot or cubic meter). Some jurisdictions, such as Saskatchewan, sell gas by volume only. Other jurisdictions, such as Alberta, gas is sold by the energy content (GJ). In these areas, almost all meters for residential and small commercial customers measure volume (m<sup>3</sup> or ft<sup>3</sup>), and billing statements include a multiplier to convert the volume to energy content of the local gas supply.
</p><p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigajoule" title="Gigajoule">gigajoule</a> (GJ) is a measure approximately equal to 80 litres (0.5 barrels) of oil, or 28 m<sup>3</sup> or 1,000 cu ft or 1 million BTUs of gas. The energy content of gas supply in Canada can vary from 37 to 43 MJ/m<sup>3</sup> (990 to 1,150 BTU/cu ft) depending on gas supply and processing between the wellhead and the customer.
</p>
<h2><span id="Adsorbed_natural_gas_.28ANG.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Adsorbed_natural_gas_(ANG)">Adsorbed natural gas (ANG)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Adsorbed natural gas (ANG)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Natural gas may be stored by adsorbing it to the porous solids called sorbents. The optimal condition for methane storage is at room temperature and atmospheric pressure. Pressures up to 4 MPa (about 40 times atmospheric pressure) will yield greater storage capacity. The most common sorbent used for ANG is activated carbon (AC), primarily in three forms: Activated Carbon Fiber (ACF), Powdered Activated Carbon (PAC), and activated carbon monolith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup>
</p>
